       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_(mythology)" title="Enceladus (mythology)">
        Enceladus (mythology)
       </a>
       . For the ship, see
       <a href="/wiki/USS_Enceladus_(AK-80)" title="USS Enceladus (AK-80)">
        USS Enceladus (AK-80)
       </a>
       .

        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchronous_rotation" title="Synchronous rotation">
          Synchronous
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">

        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_chemistry#Atmospheric_composition" title="Atmospheric chemistry">

         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         91%
         <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
          water
         </a>
         vapor
         <br/>
         4%
         <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
          nitrogen
         </a>
         <br/>
         3.2%
         <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
          carbon dioxide
         </a>
         <br/>
         1.7%
         <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
          methane
         </a>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waite_13-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Waite-13">
           [11]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Enceladus
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">
            ɛ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
           <span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">
            ɛ
           </span>
           <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
            l
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
        <i title="English pronunciation respelling">
         en-
         <span style="font-size:90%">
          SEL
         </span>
         -ə-dəs
        </i>
       </a>
       ) is the sixth-largest
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">
        moon of
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       . It is about 500 kilometers (310 mi) in diameter,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Facts_5-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Facts-5">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       about a tenth of that of Saturn's largest moon,
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       . Enceladus is mostly covered by fresh, clean ice, making it one of the most reflective bodies of the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       . Consequently, its surface temperature at noon only reaches −198 °C (−324 °F), far colder than a light-absorbing body would be. Despite its small size, Enceladus has a wide range of surface features, ranging from old, heavily
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        cratered
       </a>
       regions to young,
       <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">
        tectonically deformed
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">
        terrains
       </a>
       that formed as recently as 100 million years ago.
      </p>
      <p>
       Enceladus was discovered on August 28, 1789, by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">
        William Herschel
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discovery_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Discovery-2">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herschel_1790_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Herschel_1790-16">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herschel_1795_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Herschel_1795-17">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but little was known about it until the two
       <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">
        Voyager
       </a>
       spacecraft,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">
         Voyager 1
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">
         Voyager 2
        </a>
       </i>
       , passed nearby in the early 1980s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lovett_cosmos_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lovett_cosmos-18">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2005, the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
         Cassini
        </a>
       </i>
       spacecraft started multiple close flybys of Enceladus, revealing its surface and environment in greater detail. In particular,
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       discovered water-rich
       <a href="/wiki/Plume_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Plume (fluid dynamics)">
        plumes
       </a>
       venting from the south
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">
        polar region
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150315_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150315-19">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">
        Cryovolcanoes
       </a>
       near the south pole shoot
       <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">
        geyser
       </a>
       -like jets of
       <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">
        water vapor
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_hydrogen" title="Molecular hydrogen">
        molecular hydrogen
       </a>
       , other volatiles, and solid material, including
       <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">
        sodium chloride
       </a>
       crystals and ice particles, into space, totaling about 200 kg (440 lb) per second.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hansen-15">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lovett_cosmos_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lovett_cosmos-18">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer2013a_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spencer2013a-20">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Over 100 geysers have been identified.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140728_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140728-21">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some of the water vapor falls back as "snow"; the rest escapes, and supplies most of the material making up
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#E_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">
        Saturn's E ring
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E_ring_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-E_ring-22">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E_ring_2006_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-E_ring_2006-23">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       scientists, the plumes are similar in composition to
       <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">
        comets
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NS-20080326_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NS-20080326-24">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2014, NASA reported that
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       found evidence for a large south polar
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsurface_ocean" title="Subsurface ocean">
        subsurface ocean
       </a>
       of liquid water with a thickness of around 10 km (6 mi).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140403_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140403-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witze2014_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Witze2014-26">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140404_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140404-27">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       These geyser observations, along with the finding of escaping
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_heat" title="Internal heat">
        internal heat
       </a>
       and very few (if any) impact craters in the south polar region, show that Enceladus is currently geologically active. Like many other satellites in the extensive systems of the
       <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">
        giant planets
       </a>
       , Enceladus is trapped in an
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
        orbital resonance
       </a>
       . Its resonance with
       <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">
        Dione
       </a>
       excites its
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">
        orbital eccentricity
       </a>
       , which is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Damping" title="Damping">
        damped
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">
        tidal forces
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">
        tidally heating
       </a>
       its interior and driving the geological activity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Efroimsky_2018_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Efroimsky_2018-28">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           Further reading
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33">
         <a href="#External_links">

          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </div>
      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">
        Discovery
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_from_Voyager.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1004" data-file-width="1004" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Enceladus_from_Voyager.jpg/220px-Enceladus_from_Voyager.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Enceladus_from_Voyager.jpg/330px-Enceladus_from_Voyager.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Enceladus_from_Voyager.jpg/440px-Enceladus_from_Voyager.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_from_Voyager.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Voyager 2
         </i>
         view of Enceladus in 1981:
         <a href="/wiki/Samarkand_Sulci" title="Samarkand Sulci">
          Samarkand Sulci
         </a>
         vertical grooves (lower center);
         <a href="/wiki/Ali_Baba_(crater)" title="Ali Baba (crater)">
          Ali Baba
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Aladdin_(crater)" title="Aladdin (crater)">
          Aladdin
         </a>
         craters (upper left)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Enceladus was discovered by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">
        William Herschel
       </a>
       on August 28, 1789, during the first use of his new 1.2 m (47 in) telescope, then the largest in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herschel_1795_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Herschel_1795-17">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its faint
       <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">
        apparent magnitude
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        H
        <sub>
         V
        </sub>
       </i>
       = +11.7) and its proximity to the much brighter Saturn and Saturn's rings make Enceladus difficult to observe from Earth with smaller telescopes. Like many satellites of Saturn discovered prior to the
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Age" title="Space Age">
        Space Age
       </a>
       , Enceladus was first observed during a Saturnian equinox, when Earth is within the ring plane. At such times, the reduction in glare from the rings makes the moons easier to observe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SP-20130405_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-SP-20130405-3">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Prior to the
       <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">
        <i>
         Voyager
        </i>
        missions
       </a>
       the view of Enceladus improved little from the dot first observed by Herschel. Only its orbital characteristics were known, with estimations of its
       <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">
        mass
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">
        density
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        albedo
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">
        Naming
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Naming">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Enceladus is named after the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigantes" title="Gigantes">
        giant
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_(mythology)" title="Enceladus (mythology)">
        Enceladus
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">
        Greek mythology
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discovery_2-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Discovery-2">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The name, like the names of each of the first seven satellites of Saturn to be discovered, was suggested by William Herschel's son
       <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">
        John Herschel
       </a>
       in his 1847 publication
       <i>
        Results of Astronomical Observations made at the Cape of Good Hope
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lassell_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lassell-30">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He chose these names because
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">
        Saturn
       </a>
       , known in Greek mythology as
       <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">
        Cronus
       </a>
       , was the leader of the
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">
        Titans
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Features on Enceladus are named by the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">
        International Astronomical Union
       </a>
       (IAU) after characters and places from
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Francis_Burton" title="Richard Francis Burton">
        Burton
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_the_Thousand_Nights_and_a_Night" title="The Book of the Thousand Nights and a Night">
        translation
       </a>
       of
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Book_of_One_Thousand_and_One_Nights" title="The Book of One Thousand and One Nights">
         The Book of One Thousand and One Nights
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NameCategories_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NameCategories-31">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        Impact craters
       </a>
       are named after characters, whereas other feature types, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Fossa_(geology)" title="Fossa (geology)">
        fossae
       </a>
       (long, narrow depressions),
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorsum_(astrogeology)" title="Dorsum (astrogeology)">
        dorsa
       </a>
       (ridges),
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planitia" title="Planitia">
        planitia
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Plain" title="Plain">
        plains
       </a>
       ), and
       <a href="/wiki/Sulcus_(geology)" title="Sulcus (geology)">
        sulci
       </a>
       (long parallel grooves), are named after places. The IAU has officially named 85 features on Enceladus, most recently Samaria Rupes, formerly called Samaria Fossa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">
        Orbit and rotation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Orbit and rotation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_orbit_2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="632" data-file-width="632" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Enceladus_orbit_2.jpg/220px-Enceladus_orbit_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Enceladus_orbit_2.jpg/330px-Enceladus_orbit_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Enceladus_orbit_2.jpg/440px-Enceladus_orbit_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_orbit_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         View of Enceladus's orbit (highlighted in red) from above Saturn's north pole
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Enceladus is one of the major inner satellites of Saturn along with
       <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">
        Dione
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">
        Tethys
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">
        Mimas
       </a>
       . It orbits at 238,000 km from Saturn's center and 180,000 km from its cloud tops, between the orbits of Mimas and Tethys. It orbits Saturn every 32.9 hours, fast enough for its motion to be observed over a single night of observation. Enceladus is currently in a 2:1 mean-motion
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
        orbital resonance
       </a>
       with Dione, completing two orbits around Saturn for every one orbit completed by Dione. This resonance maintains Enceladus's orbital eccentricity (0.0047), which is known as a forced eccentricity. This non-zero eccentricity results in tidal deformation of Enceladus. The dissipated heat resulting from this deformation is the main heating source for Enceladus's geologic activity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Enceladus orbits within the densest part of
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#E_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">
        Saturn's E ring
       </a>
       , the outermost of
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">
        its major rings
       </a>
       , and is the main source of the ring's material composition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ras.377_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ras.377-34">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Like most of Saturn's larger satellites, Enceladus rotates synchronously with its orbital period, keeping one face pointed toward Saturn. Unlike Earth's
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       , Enceladus does not appear to
       <a href="/wiki/Libration" title="Libration">
        librate
       </a>
       more than 1.5° about its spin axis. However, analysis of the shape of Enceladus suggests that at some point it was in a 1:4 forced secondary spin–orbit libration.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This libration could have provided Enceladus with an additional heat source.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvard_2008_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Harvard_2008-35">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Source_of_the_E_ring">
        Source of the E ring
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Source of the E ring">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Plumes from Enceladus, which are similar in composition to comets,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NS-20080326_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NS-20080326-24">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       have been shown to be the source of the material in Saturn's
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#E_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">
        E ring
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E_ring_22-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-E_ring-22">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The E ring is the widest and outermost ring of Saturn (except for the tenuous
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoebe_ring" title="Phoebe ring">
        Phoebe ring
       </a>
       ). It is an extremely wide but diffuse disk of microscopic icy or dusty material distributed between the orbits of
       <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">
        Mimas
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hedman_2012_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hedman_2012-36">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Mathematical models show that the E ring is unstable, with a lifespan between 10,000 and 1,000,000 years; therefore, particles composing it must be constantly replenished.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csa.cassini_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-csa.cassini-37">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Enceladus is orbiting inside the ring, at its narrowest but highest density point. In the 1980s some suspected that Enceladus is the main source of particles for the ring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baum_1981_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Baum_1981-38">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haff_1983_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Haff_1983-39">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref.09459_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ref.09459-40">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssu.241_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ssu.241-41">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This hypothesis was confirmed by
       <i>
        Cassini's
       </i>
       first two close flybys in 2005.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spahn_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spahn-42">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ut.12710_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ut.12710-43">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>

      <div class="center">
       <div class="thumb tnone">
        <div class="thumbinner" style="width:602px;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19061-SaturnMoonEnceladus-CurtainNotDiscrete-Eruptions-20150506.jpg">
          <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1022" data-file-width="2046" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/PIA19061-SaturnMoonEnceladus-CurtainNotDiscrete-Eruptions-20150506.jpg/600px-PIA19061-SaturnMoonEnceladus-CurtainNotDiscrete-Eruptions-20150506.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/PIA19061-SaturnMoonEnceladus-CurtainNotDiscrete-Eruptions-20150506.jpg/900px-PIA19061-SaturnMoonEnceladus-CurtainNotDiscrete-Eruptions-20150506.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/PIA19061-SaturnMoonEnceladus-CurtainNotDiscrete-Eruptions-20150506.jpg/1200px-PIA19061-SaturnMoonEnceladus-CurtainNotDiscrete-Eruptions-20150506.jpg 2x" width="600"/>
         </a>
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          <div class="magnify">
           <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19061-SaturnMoonEnceladus-CurtainNotDiscrete-Eruptions-20150506.jpg" title="Enlarge">
           </a>
          </div>
          <center>
           Eruptions on Enceladus look like "discrete" jets, but may be "curtain" eruptions instead
           <br/>
           (
           <a class="external autonumber" href="http://photojournal.jpl.nasa.gov/archive/PIA19060_JPL-20150313-pCassin-0001-640.mp4" rel="nofollow">
            [1]
           </a>
           video animation)
          </center>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">
        Geology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_features">
        Surface features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Surface features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_geological_features_on_Enceladus" title="List of geological features on Enceladus">
        List of geological features on Enceladus
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18362-SaturnMoonEnceladus-Plains-20160114.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1020" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/PIA18362-SaturnMoonEnceladus-Plains-20160114.jpg/150px-PIA18362-SaturnMoonEnceladus-Plains-20160114.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/PIA18362-SaturnMoonEnceladus-Plains-20160114.jpg/225px-PIA18362-SaturnMoonEnceladus-Plains-20160114.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/PIA18362-SaturnMoonEnceladus-Plains-20160114.jpg/300px-PIA18362-SaturnMoonEnceladus-Plains-20160114.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18362-SaturnMoonEnceladus-Plains-20160114.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Enceladus – tilted
         <a href="/wiki/Terminator_(solar)" title="Terminator (solar)">
          terminator
         </a>
         – north is up
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">
         Voyager 2
        </a>
       </i>
       was the first spacecraft to observe Enceladus's surface in detail, in August 1981. Examination of the resulting highest-resolution imagery revealed at least five different types of terrain, including several regions of cratered terrain, regions of smooth (young) terrain, and lanes of ridged terrain often bordering the smooth areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothery_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothery-44">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition, extensive linear cracks
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cracks_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cracks-45">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">
        scarps
       </a>
       were observed. Given the relative lack of craters on the smooth plains, these regions are probably less than a few hundred million years old. Accordingly, Enceladus must have been recently active with "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryovolcanism" title="Cryovolcanism">
        water volcanism
       </a>
       " or other processes that renew the surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solstice_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Solstice-46">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The fresh, clean ice that dominates its surface gives Enceladus the most reflective surface of any body in the Solar System, with a visual
       <a href="/wiki/Geometric_albedo" title="Geometric albedo">
        geometric albedo
       </a>
       of 1.38
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verbiscer_et_al._2007_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Verbiscer_et_al._2007-9">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and bolometric
       <a href="/wiki/Bond_albedo" title="Bond albedo">
        Bond albedo
       </a>
       of
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         6999810000000000000♠
        </span>
        0.81
        <span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ±
        </span>
        0.04
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howett_2010_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Howett_2010-10">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because it reflects so much sunlight, its surface only reaches a mean noon temperature of −198 °C (−324 °F), somewhat colder than other Saturnian satellites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spencer-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Observations during three flybys by
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       on February 17, March 9, and July 14, 2005, revealed Enceladus' surface features in much greater detail than the
       <i>
        Voyager 2
       </i>
       observations. The smooth plains, which
       <i>
        Voyager 2
       </i>
       had observed, resolved into relatively crater-free regions filled with numerous small
       <a href="/wiki/Ridge" title="Ridge">
        ridges
       </a>
       and scarps. Numerous fractures were found within the older, cratered terrain, suggesting that the surface has been subjected to extensive deformation since the craters were formed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rathbun_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rathbun-47">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some areas contain no craters, indicating major resurfacing events in the geologically recent past. There are fissures, plains, corrugated terrain and other crustal deformations. Several additional regions of young terrain were discovered in areas not well-imaged by either
       <i>
        Voyager
       </i>
       spacecraft, such as the bizarre terrain near the south pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       All of this indicates that Enceladus's interior may be liquid today, even though it should have been frozen long ago.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solstice_46-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Solstice-46">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <center>
