      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_craters">
        Impact craters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Impact craters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        Impact cratering
       </a>
       is a common occurrence on many Solar System bodies. Much of Enceladus' surface is covered with craters at various densities and levels of degradation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith-48">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This subdivision of cratered terrains on the basis of crater density (and thus surface age) suggests that Enceladus has been resurfaced in multiple stages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solstice_46-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Solstice-46">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       observations provided a much closer look at the crater distribution and size, showing that many of Enceladus' craters are heavily degraded through viscous relaxation and
       <a href="/wiki/Fracture" title="Fracture">
        fracturing
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turtle_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Turtle-49">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Viscous relaxation allows gravity, over geologic time scales, to deform craters and other topographic features formed in water ice, reducing the amount of topography over time. The rate at which this occurs is dependent on the temperature of the ice: warmer ice is easier to deform than colder, stiffer ice. Viscously relaxed craters tend to have
       <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">
        domed
       </a>
       floors, or are recognized as craters only by a raised, circular rim.
       <a href="/wiki/Dunyazad_(crater)" title="Dunyazad (crater)">
        Dunyazad crater
       </a>
       is a prime example of a viscously relaxed crater on Enceladus, with a prominent domed floor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Se-4_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Se-4-50">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tectonic_features">
        Tectonic features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Tectonic features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_PIA06191.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2876" data-file-width="3750" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Enceladus_PIA06191.jpg/200px-Enceladus_PIA06191.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Enceladus_PIA06191.jpg/300px-Enceladus_PIA06191.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Enceladus_PIA06191.jpg/400px-Enceladus_PIA06191.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_PIA06191.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         View of Enceladus's
         <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">
          Europa
         </a>
         -like surface with the
         <a href="/wiki/Labtayt_Sulci" title="Labtayt Sulci">
          Labtayt Sulci
         </a>
         fractures at center and the Ebony and Cufa dorsa at lower left, imaged by
         <i>
          Cassini
         </i>
         on February 17, 2005
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        Voyager 2
       </i>
       found several types of tectonic features on Enceladus, including
       <a href="/wiki/Trough_(geology)" title="Trough (geology)">
        troughs
       </a>
       , scarps, and
       <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">
        belts
       </a>
       of grooves and
       <a href="/wiki/Ridge" title="Ridge">
        ridges
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothery_44-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothery-44">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Results from
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       suggest that
       <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">
        tectonics
       </a>
       is the dominant mode of deformation on Enceladus, including rifts, one of the more dramatic types of tectonic features that were noted. These canyons can be up to 200 km long, 5–10 km wide, and 1 km deep. Such features are geologically young, because they cut across other tectonic features and have sharp topographic relief with prominent outcrops along the cliff faces.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LPS_2006_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LPS_2006-51">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Evidence of tectonics on Enceladus is also derived from grooved terrain, consisting of lanes of curvilinear grooves and ridges. These bands, first discovered by
       <i>
        Voyager 2
       </i>
       , often separate smooth plains from cratered regions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothery_44-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothery-44">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Grooved terrains such as the Samarkand Sulci are reminiscent of grooved terrain on
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        Ganymede
       </a>
       . However, unlike those seen on Ganymede, grooved topography on Enceladus is generally more complex. Rather than parallel sets of grooves, these lanes often appear as bands of crudely aligned, chevron-shaped features. In other areas, these bands bow upwards with fractures and ridges running the length of the feature.
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       observations of the Samarkand Sulci have revealed dark spots (125 and 750 m wide) located parallel to the narrow fractures. Currently, these spots are interpreted as collapse pits within these ridged plain belts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turtle_49-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Turtle-49">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition to deep fractures and grooved lanes, Enceladus has several other types of tectonic terrain. Many of these fractures are found in bands cutting across cratered terrain. These fractures probably propagate down only a few hundred meters into the crust. Many have probably been influenced during their formation by the weakened
       <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
        regolith
       </a>
       produced by impact craters, often changing the strike of the propagating fracture.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turtle_49-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Turtle-49">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Another example of tectonic features on Enceladus are the linear grooves first found by
       <i>
        Voyager 2
       </i>
       and seen at a much higher resolution by
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       . These linear grooves can be seen cutting across other terrain types, like the groove and ridge belts. Like the deep rifts, they are among the youngest features on Enceladus. However, some linear grooves have been softened like the craters nearby, suggesting that they are older. Ridges have also been observed on Enceladus, though not nearly to the extent as those seen on
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">
        Europa
       </a>
       . These ridges are relatively limited in extent and are up to one kilometer tall. One-kilometer high domes have also been observed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turtle_49-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Turtle-49">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Given the level of resurfacing found on Enceladus, it is clear that tectonic movement has been an important driver of geology for much of its history.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LPS_2006_51-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-LPS_2006-51">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Smooth_plains">
        Smooth plains
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Smooth plains">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Two regions of smooth plains were observed by
       <i>
        Voyager 2
       </i>
       . They generally have low relief and have far fewer craters than in the cratered terrains, indicating a relatively young surface age.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_48-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith-48">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In one of the smooth plain regions,
       <a href="/wiki/Sarandib_Planitia" title="Sarandib Planitia">
        Sarandib Planitia
       </a>
       , no impact craters were visible down to the limit of resolution. Another region of smooth plains to the southwest of Sarandib is criss-crossed by several troughs and scarps.
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       has since viewed these smooth plains regions, like Sarandib Planitia and
       <a href="/wiki/Diyar_Planitia" title="Diyar Planitia">
        Diyar Planitia
       </a>
       at much higher resolution.
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       images show these regions filled with low-relief ridges and fractures, probably caused by
       <a href="/wiki/Shear_stress" title="Shear stress">
        shear deformation
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turtle_49-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Turtle-49">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The high-resolution images of Sarandib Planitia revealed a number of small impact craters, which allow for an estimate of the surface age, either 170 million years or 3.7
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" title="1000000000 (number)">
        billion
       </a>
       years, depending on assumed impactor population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crater_flux_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-crater_flux-53">
         [c]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The expanded surface coverage provided by
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       has allowed for the identification of additional regions of smooth plains, particularly on Enceladus's leading hemisphere (the side of Enceladus that faces the direction of motion as it orbits Saturn). Rather than being covered in low-relief ridges, this region is covered in numerous criss-crossing sets of troughs and ridges, similar to the deformation seen in the south polar region. This area is on the opposite side of Enceladus from Sarandib and Diyar Planitiae, suggesting that the placement of these regions is influenced by Saturn's tides on Enceladus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pappalardo_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pappalardo-54">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="South_polar_region">
        South polar region
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: South polar region">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Tiger_stripes_(Enceladus)" title="Tiger stripes (Enceladus)">
        Tiger stripes (Enceladus)
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_ultra_closeup.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1024" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2a/Enceladus_ultra_closeup.jpg/200px-Enceladus_ultra_closeup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2a/Enceladus_ultra_closeup.jpg/300px-Enceladus_ultra_closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2a/Enceladus_ultra_closeup.jpg/400px-Enceladus_ultra_closeup.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_ultra_closeup.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Close-up of south pole terrain
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Images taken by
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       during the flyby on July 14, 2005, revealed a distinctive, tectonically deformed region surrounding Enceladus's south pole. This area, reaching as far north as 60° south latitude, is covered in tectonic fractures and ridges.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ciclops1223_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ciclops1223-55">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The area has few sizable impact craters, suggesting that it is the youngest surface on Enceladus and on any of the mid-sized icy satellites; modeling of the cratering rate suggests that some regions of the south polar terrain are possibly as young as 500,000 years or less.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Near the center of this terrain are four fractures bounded by ridges, unofficially called "
       <a href="/wiki/Tiger_stripes_(Enceladus)" title="Tiger stripes (Enceladus)">
        tiger stripes
       </a>
       ". They appear to be the youngest features in this region and are surrounded by mint-green-colored (in false color, UV–green–near IR images), coarse-grained water ice, seen elsewhere on the surface within outcrops and fracture walls.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ciclops1223_55-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ciclops1223-55">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Here the "blue" ice is on a flat surface, indicating that the region is young enough not to have been coated by fine-grained water ice from the
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#E_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">
        E ring
       </a>
       . Results from the visual and infrared spectrometer (VIMS) instrument suggest that the green-colored material surrounding the tiger stripes is chemically distinct from the rest of the surface of Enceladus. VIMS detected crystalline water ice in the stripes, suggesting that they are quite young (likely less than 1,000 years old) or the surface ice has been thermally altered in the recent past.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIMS_PR_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIMS_PR-56">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       VIMS also detected simple organic (carbon-containing) compounds in the tiger stripes, chemistry not found anywhere else on Enceladus thus far.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Brown-57">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One of these areas of "blue" ice in the south polar region was observed at high resolution during the July 14 flyby, revealing an area of extreme tectonic deformation and blocky terrain, with some areas covered in boulders 10–100 m across.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ciclops1250_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ciclops1250-58">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18366-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Yshaped-20160215.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="947" data-file-width="1019" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/PIA18366-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Yshaped-20160215.jpg/200px-PIA18366-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Yshaped-20160215.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/PIA18366-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Yshaped-20160215.jpg/300px-PIA18366-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Yshaped-20160215.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/PIA18366-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Yshaped-20160215.jpg/400px-PIA18366-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Yshaped-20160215.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18366-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Yshaped-20160215.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Y-shaped discontinuities, imaged February 15, 2016
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The boundary of the south polar region is marked by a pattern of parallel, Y- and V-shaped ridges and valleys. The shape, orientation, and location of these features suggest they are caused by changes in the overall shape of Enceladus. As of 2006 there were two theories for what could cause such a shift in shape: the orbit of Enceladus may have migrated inward, leading to an increase in Enceladus's rotation rate. Such a shift would lead to a more oblate shape;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       or a rising mass of warm, low-density material in Enceladus's interior may have led to a shift in the position of the current south polar terrain from Enceladus's southern mid-latitudes to its south pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pappalardo_54-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pappalardo-54">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Consequently, the moon's ellipsoid shape would have adjusted to match the new orientation. One problem of the polar flattening hypothesis is that both polar regions should have similar tectonic deformation histories.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the north polar region is densely cratered, and has a much older surface age than the south pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_48-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith-48">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thickness variations in Enceladus's
       <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">
        lithosphere
       </a>
       is one explanation for this discrepancy. Variations in lithospheric thickness are supported by the correlation between the Y-shaped discontinuities and the V-shaped cusps along the south polar terrain margin and the relative surface age of the adjacent non-south polar terrain regions. The Y-shaped discontinuities, and the north-south trending tension fractures into which they lead, are correlated with younger terrain with presumably thinner lithospheres. The V-shaped cusps are adjacent to older, more heavily cratered terrains.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">
        Internal structure
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Internal structure">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_Roll.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="1536" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Enceladus_Roll.jpg/200px-Enceladus_Roll.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Enceladus_Roll.jpg/300px-Enceladus_Roll.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Enceladus_Roll.jpg/400px-Enceladus_Roll.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_Roll.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A model of the interior of Enceladus: silicate core (brown); water-ice-rich mantle (white); a proposed
         <a href="/wiki/Diapir" title="Diapir">
          diapir
         </a>
         under the south pole (noted in the mantle (yellow) and core (red))
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pappalardo_54-2">
          <a href="#cite_note-Pappalardo-54">
           [51]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Before the
       <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
        <i>
         Cassini
        </i>
       </a>
       mission, little was known about the interior of Enceladus. However, flybys by
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       provided information for models of Enceladus's interior, including a better determination of the mass and shape, high-resolution observations of the surface, and new insights on the interior.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa.usea_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-esa.usea-59">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aas.44_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-aas.44-60">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Mass estimates from the
       <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">
        Voyager program
       </a>
       missions suggested that Enceladus was composed almost entirely of water ice.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothery_44-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothery-44">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, based on the effects of Enceladus's
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">
        gravity
       </a>
       on
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       , its mass was determined to be much higher than previously thought, yielding a density of 1.61
       <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">
        g
       </a>
       /cm
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This density is higher than Saturn's other mid-sized icy satellites, indicating that Enceladus contains a greater percentage of
       <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">
        silicates
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
        iron
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Castillo et al. (2005) suggested that
       <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">
        Iapetus
       </a>
       and the other icy satellites of Saturn formed relatively quickly after the formation of the Saturnian subnebula, and thus were rich in short-lived radionuclides.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castillo1_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Castillo1-61">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhatia2017_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bhatia2017-62">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       . These radionuclides, like
       <a href="/wiki/Aluminium#Isotopes" title="Aluminium">
        aluminium-26
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Iron#Isotopes" title="Iron">
        iron-60
       </a>
       , have short half-lives and would produce interior heating relatively quickly. Without the short-lived variety, Enceladus's complement of long-lived radionuclides would not have been enough to prevent rapid freezing of the interior, even with Enceladus's comparatively high rock–mass fraction, given its small size.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castillo2_63-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Castillo2-63">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Given Enceladus's relatively high rock–mass fraction, the proposed enhancement in
       <sup>
        26
       </sup>
       Al and
       <sup>
        60
       </sup>
       Fe would result in a
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">
        differentiated
       </a>
       body, with an icy
       <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">
        mantle
       </a>
       and a rocky
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">
        core
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schubert2007_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schubert2007-64">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhatia2017_62-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bhatia2017-62">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Subsequent radioactive and
       <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">
        tidal
       </a>
       heating would raise the temperature of the core to 1,000 K, enough to melt the inner mantle. However, for Enceladus to still be active, part of the core must have also melted, forming
       <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">
        magma
       </a>
       chambers that would flex under the strain of Saturn's tides. Tidal heating, such as from the resonance with Dione or from
       <a href="/wiki/Libration" title="Libration">
        libration
       </a>
       , would then have sustained these hot spots in the core and would power the current geological activity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvard_2008_35-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Harvard_2008-35">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matson_65-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Matson-65">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition to its mass and modeled
       <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">
        geochemistry
       </a>
       , researchers have also examined Enceladus's shape to determine if it is differentiated. Porco et al. (2006) used limb measurements to determine that its shape, assuming
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">
        hydrostatic equilibrium
       </a>
       , is consistent with an undifferentiated interior, in contradiction to the geological and geochemical evidence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the current shape also supports the possibility that Enceladus is not in hydrostatic equilibrium, and may have rotated faster at some point in the recent past (with a differentiated interior).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schubert2007_64-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schubert2007-64">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Gravity measurements by
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       show that the density of the core is low, indicating that the core contains water in addition to silicates.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taubner_et_al._2014_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Taubner_et_al._2014-66">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Subsurface_water_ocean">
        Subsurface water ocean
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Subsurface water ocean">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="4000" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg/250px-PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg/375px-PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg/500px-PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Artist's impression of possible hydrothermal activity on Enceladus's ocean floor
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
          <a href="#cite_note-67">
           [64]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2812" data-file-width="5000" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg/300px-PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg/450px-PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg/600px-PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19656-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-Ocean-ArtConcept-20150915.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Artist's impression of a global subsurface ocean of liquid water
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140403_25-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140403-25">
           [23]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140404_27-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140404-27">
           [25]
          </a>
         </sup>
         (
         <a href="/wiki/File:PIA20013-Enceladus-SaturnMoon-ArtistConcept-20151026.jpg" title="File:PIA20013-Enceladus-SaturnMoon-ArtistConcept-20151026.jpg">
          updated and better scaled version
         </a>
         )
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Evidence of liquid water on Enceladus began to accumulate in 2005, when scientists observed plumes containing water vapor spewing from its south polar surface,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rain_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rain-68">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with jets moving 250 kg of water vapor every second
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rain_68-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rain-68">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       at up to 2,189 km/h (1,360 mph) into space.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news9.9422981_69-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-news9.9422981-69">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Soon after, in 2006 it was determined that Enceladus's plumes are the source of Saturn's
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#E_ring" title="Rings of Saturn">
        E Ring
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spahn_42-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spahn-42">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The sources of salty particles are uniformly distributed along the
       <a href="/wiki/Tiger_stripes_(Enceladus)" title="Tiger stripes (Enceladus)">
        tiger stripes
       </a>
       , whereas sources of "fresh" particles are closely related to the high-speed gas jets. The "salty" particles are heavier and mostly fall back to the surface, whereas the fast "fresh" particles escape to the E ring, explaining its salt-poor composition of 0.5–2% of sodium salts by mass.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Postberg2011_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Postberg2011-70">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The plumes' "salty" composition indicates that the source is a salty
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsurface_ocean" title="Subsurface ocean">
        subsurface ocean
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.090624_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-space.090624-71">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       also found traces of simple
       <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">
        organic compounds
       </a>
       in some dust grains,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Postberg2011_70-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Postberg2011-70">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa.037PG_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-esa.037PG-72">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as well as complex organics up to
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        C
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         6
        </span>
        H
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         6
        </span>
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Gravimetric data from
       <i>
        Cassini'
       </i>
       s December 2010 flybys showed that Enceladus likely has a liquid water ocean beneath its frozen surface, but at the time it was thought the subsurface ocean was limited to the south pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140403_25-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140403-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witze2014_26-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Witze2014-26">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140404_27-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140404-27">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ocean_Apr_2014_74-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ocean_Apr_2014-74">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The top of the ocean probably lies beneath a 30 to 40 kilometers (19 to 25 mi) thick ice shelf. The ocean may be 10 kilometers (6.2 mi) deep at the south pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140403_25-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140403-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian-20140403_75-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Guardian-20140403-75">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Measurements of Enceladus's "wobble" as it orbits Saturn—called
       <a href="/wiki/Libration" title="Libration">
        libration
       </a>
       —suggests that the entire icy crust is detached from the rocky core and therefore that a global ocean is present beneath the surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-global_76-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-global-76">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The amount of libration (0.120° ± 0.014°) implies that this global ocean is about 26 to 31 kilometers deep.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-Global_78-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-Global-78">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciam_79-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sciam-79">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For comparison, Earth's ocean has an average depth of 3.7 kilometers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciam_79-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-sciam-79">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A model suggests that Enceladus's salty ocean (-Na, -Cl, -CO
       <sub>
        3
       </sub>
       ) has an alkaline
       <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">
        pH
       </a>
       of 11 to 12.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pH_2015_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pH_2015-81">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glein_2015_82-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Glein_2015-82">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The high pH is interpreted to be a consequence of
       <a href="/wiki/Serpentinite" title="Serpentinite">
        serpentinization
       </a>
       of chondritic rock that leads to the generation of H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       , a geochemical source of energy that can support both abiotic and biological synthesis of organic molecules such as those that have been detected in Enceladus's plumes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pH_2015_81-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-pH_2015-81">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall_83-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wall-83">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="South_polar_plumes">
        South polar plumes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: South polar plumes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">
        Cryovolcano
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_Cold_Geyser_Model.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2880" data-file-width="3840" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Enceladus_Cold_Geyser_Model.svg/200px-Enceladus_Cold_Geyser_Model.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Enceladus_Cold_Geyser_Model.svg/300px-Enceladus_Cold_Geyser_Model.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Enceladus_Cold_Geyser_Model.svg/400px-Enceladus_Cold_Geyser_Model.svg.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_Cold_Geyser_Model.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         One possible scheme for Enceladus's cryovolcanism
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Following
       <i>
        Voyager's
       </i>
       encounters with Enceladus in the early 1980s, scientists postulated that it may be geologically active based on its young, reflective surface and location near the core of the E ring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothery_44-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothery-44">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Based on the connection between Enceladus and the E ring, scientists suspected that Enceladus was the source of material in the E ring, perhaps through venting of water vapor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baum_1981_38-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Baum_1981-38">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haff_1983_39-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Haff_1983-39">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Readings from
       <i>
        Cassini's
       </i>
       2005 passage suggested that
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryovolcanism" title="Cryovolcanism">
        cryovolcanism
       </a>
       , where water and other volatiles are the materials erupted instead of silicate rock, had been discovered on Enceladus. The first
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       sighting of a plume of icy particles above Enceladus's south pole came from the Imaging Science Subsystem (ISS) images taken in January and February 2005,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       though the possibility of a camera artifact delayed an official announcement. Data from the
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">
        magnetometer
       </a>
       instrument during the February 17, 2005, encounter provided a hint that the feature might be real when it found evidence for a planetary atmosphere. The magnetometer observed an increase in the power of
       <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_ion_cyclotron_wave" title="Electrostatic ion cyclotron wave">
        ion cyclotron waves
       </a>
       near Enceladus. These waves are produced by the interaction of ionized particles and magnetic fields, and the waves' frequency can be used to identify their composition, in this case
       <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">
        ionized
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">
        water vapor
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dougherty_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dougherty-14">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During the two following encounters, the
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">
        magnetometer
       </a>
       team determined that gases in Enceladus's atmosphere are concentrated over the south polar region, with atmospheric density away from the pole being much lower.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dougherty_14-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Dougherty-14">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
        Ultraviolet
       </a>
       Imaging Spectrograph (UVIS) confirmed this result by observing two
       <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">
        stellar occultations
       </a>
       during the February 17 and July 14 encounters. Unlike the magnetometer, UVIS failed to detect an atmosphere above Enceladus during the February encounter when it looked over the equatorial region, but did detect water vapor during an occultation over the south polar region during the July encounter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_15-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hansen-15">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_plume_molecules.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1440" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Enceladus_plume_molecules.jpg/250px-Enceladus_plume_molecules.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Enceladus_plume_molecules.jpg/375px-Enceladus_plume_molecules.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Enceladus_plume_molecules.jpg/500px-Enceladus_plume_molecules.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_plume_molecules.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Chemical composition of Enceladus's plumes
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Fortuitously,
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       flew through this gas cloud during the July 14 encounter, allowing instruments such as the ion and neutral
       <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">
        mass spectrometer
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/INMS" title="INMS">
        INMS
       </a>
       ) and the cosmic dust analyzer (CDA) to directly sample the plume. INMS measured the composition of the gas cloud, detecting mostly
       <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">
        water vapor
       </a>
       , as well as traces of molecular
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
        nitrogen
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        methane
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waite_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Waite-13">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The CDA "detected a large increase in the number of particles near Enceladus", confirming Enceladus as the primary source for the E ring.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spahn_42-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spahn-42">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Analysis of the CDA and INMS data suggest that the gas cloud
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       flew through during the July encounter, and observed from a distance with its magnetometer and UVIS, was actually a water-rich cryovolcanic plume, originating from vents near the south pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL619_84-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-JPL619-84">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19059-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-OceanMethane-20150311.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3906" data-file-width="4820" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/PIA19059-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-OceanMethane-20150311.png/250px-PIA19059-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-OceanMethane-20150311.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/PIA19059-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-OceanMethane-20150311.png/375px-PIA19059-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-OceanMethane-20150311.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/PIA19059-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-OceanMethane-20150311.png/500px-PIA19059-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-OceanMethane-20150311.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19059-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-OceanMethane-20150311.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Possible origins of methane found in plumes
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Visual confirmation of venting came in November 2005, when ISS imaged
       <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">
        geyser
       </a>
       -like
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plume_(hydrodynamics)" title="Plume (hydrodynamics)">
        jets
       </a>
       of icy particles rising from Enceladus's south polar region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-E_ring_2006_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-E_ring_2006-23">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (Although the plume was imaged before, in January and February 2005, additional studies of the camera's response at high phase angles, when the Sun is almost behind Enceladus, and comparison with equivalent high-phase-angle images taken of other Saturnian satellites, were required before this could be confirmed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ciclops1652_85-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ciclops1652-85">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) The November 2005 images showed the plume's fine structure, revealing numerous jets (perhaps issuing from numerous distinct vents) within a larger, faint component extending out nearly 500 km from the surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPL619_84-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-JPL619-84">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The particles have a bulk velocity of 1.25 ±0.1 km/s,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perry2846_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-perry2846-86">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and a maximum velocity of Mach 10 (3.40 km/s).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Cassini's
       </i>
       UVIS later observed gas jets coinciding with the dust jets seen by ISS during a non-targeted encounter with Enceladus in October 2007.
      </p>
      <p>
       Observations during a flyby on March 12, 2008, revealed additional chemicals in the plume, including trace amounts of simple hydrocarbons such as
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        methane
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">
        propane
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">
        acetylene
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">
        formaldehyde
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.5179_88-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-space.5179-88">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR03262008_89-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PR03262008-89">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The plumes' composition, as measured by the INMS, is similar to that seen at most comets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR03262008_89-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-PR03262008-89">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2015, the Cassini probe made a close fly-by of Enceladus' south pole, flying within 48.3 km (30 mi) of the surface, and through a plume. Its mass spectrometer detected
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
        molecular hydrogen
       </a>
       which was in "thermodynamic disequilibrium" with the other components.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The combined analysis of imaging, mass spectrometry, and magnetospheric data suggests that the observed south polar plume emanates from pressurized subsurface chambers, similar to Earth's
       <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">
        geysers
       </a>
       ,.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The mechanism that drives and sustains the eruptions is thought to be tidal heating.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kite_3972–3975_91-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kite_3972–3975-91">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The intensity of the eruption of the south polar jets varies significantly as a function of the position of Enceladus in its orbit. The plumes are about four times brighter when Enceladus is at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apoapsis" title="Apoapsis">
        apoapsis
       </a>
       (the point in its orbit most distant from Saturn) than when it is at
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periapsis" title="Periapsis">
        periapsis
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spotts2013_92-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spotts2013-92">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2013_93-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2013-93">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer2013b_94-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spencer2013b-94">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is consistent with geophysical calculations which predict the south polar fissures are under compression near periapsis, pushing them shut, and under tension near apoapsis, pulling them open.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HedmanGosmeyer2013_95-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-HedmanGosmeyer2013-95">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Much of the plume activity consists of broad curtain-like eruptions. Optical illusions from a combination of viewing direction and local fracture geometry previously made the plumes look like discrete jets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The extent to which
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryovolcanism" title="Cryovolcanism">
        cryovolcanism
       </a>
       really occurs is a subject of some debate, as water, being denser than ice by about 8%, has difficulty erupting under normal circumstances. At Enceladus, it appears that cryovolcanism occurs because water-filled cracks are periodically exposed to vacuum, the cracks being opened and closed by tidal stresses.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nimmo_2016_99-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nimmo_2016-99">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_heat_sources">
        Possible heat sources
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Possible heat sources">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       During the flyby of July 14, 2005, the Composite Infrared Spectrometer (CIRS) found a warm region near the south pole. Temperatures in this region ranged from 85–90 K, with small areas showing as high as 157 K (−116 °C), much too warm to be explained by solar heating, indicating that parts of the south polar region are heated from the interior of Enceladus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer_11-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spencer-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The presence of a subsurface ocean under the south polar region is now accepted,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman_2013_102-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Showman_2013-102">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but it cannot explain the source of the heat, with an estimated heat flux of 200 mW/m
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       , which is about 10 times higher than that from radiogenic heating alone.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heat_flux_2016_103-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-heat_flux_2016-103">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jet_Spots_in_Tiger_Stripes_PIA10361.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2254" data-file-width="2240" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Jet_Spots_in_Tiger_Stripes_PIA10361.jpg/200px-Jet_Spots_in_Tiger_Stripes_PIA10361.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Jet_Spots_in_Tiger_Stripes_PIA10361.jpg/300px-Jet_Spots_in_Tiger_Stripes_PIA10361.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Jet_Spots_in_Tiger_Stripes_PIA10361.jpg/400px-Jet_Spots_in_Tiger_Stripes_PIA10361.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jet_Spots_in_Tiger_Stripes_PIA10361.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Heat map of the south polar fractures, dubbed 'tiger stripes'
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Several explanations for the observed elevated temperatures and the resulting plumes have been proposed, including venting from a subsurface reservoir of liquid water,
       <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">
        sublimation
       </a>
       of ice,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DPS_meeting_#45_104-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DPS_meeting_#45-104">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       decompression and dissociation of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clathrate" title="Clathrate">
        clathrates
       </a>
       , and shear heating,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spencer_2014_105-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-spencer_2014-105">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but a complete explanation of all the heat sources causing the observed thermal power output of Enceladus has not yet been settled.
      </p>
      <p>
       Heating in Enceladus has occurred through various mechanisms ever since its formation.
       <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">
        Radioactive decay
       </a>
       in its core may have initially heated it,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Hot_Start_on_Enceladus_106-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-A_Hot_Start_on_Enceladus-106">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       giving it a warm core and a subsurface ocean, which is now kept above freezing through an unidentified mechanism. Geophysical models indicate that
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">
        tidal heating
       </a>
       is a main heat source, perhaps aided by radioactive decay and some
       <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_reaction" title="Exothermic reaction">
        heat-producing chemical reactions
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15_gigawatts_107-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-15_gigawatts-107">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sd.001237_108-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sd.001237-108">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-areps.41_109-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-areps.41-109">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icarus.226.1_110-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-icarus.226.1-110">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A 2007 study predicted the internal heat of Enceladus, if generated by tidal forces, could be no greater than 1.1 gigawatts,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPSC_2013_111-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-EPSC_2013-111">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but data from
       <i>
        Cassini's
       </i>
       infrared spectrometer of the south polar terrain over 16 months, indicate that the internal heat generated power is about 4.7 gigawatts,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPSC_2013_111-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-EPSC_2013-111">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and suggest that it is in thermal equilibrium.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer_11-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spencer-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VIMS_PR_56-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-VIMS_PR-56">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature.7163_112-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nature.7163-112">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The observed power output of 4.7 gigawatts is challenging to explain from tidal heating alone, so the main source of heat remains a mystery.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15_gigawatts_107-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-15_gigawatts-107">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most scientists think the observed heat flux of Enceladus is not enough to maintain the subsurface ocean, and therefore any subsurface ocean must be a remnant of a period of higher eccentricity and tidal heating, or the heat is produced through another mechanism.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icarus.188.2_113-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-icarus.188.2-113">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts2008_114-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Roberts2008-114">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tidal_heating">
        Tidal heating
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Tidal heating">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">
        Tidal heating
       </a>
       occurs through the tidal friction processes: orbital and rotational energy are dissipated as heat in the crust of an object. In addition, to the extent that tides produce heat along fractures,
       <a href="/wiki/Libration" title="Libration">
        libration
       </a>
       may affect the magnitude and distribution of such tidal shear heating.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvard_2008_35-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Harvard_2008-35">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Tidal dissipation of Enceladus's ice crust is significant because Enceladus has a subsurface ocean. A computer simulation that used data from
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       was published in November 2017, and it indicates that friction heat from the sliding rock fragments within the permeable and fragmented core of Enceladus could keep its underground ocean warm for up to billions of years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
        <a href="#cite_note-115">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
        <a href="#cite_note-116">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
        <a href="#cite_note-117">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is thought that if Enceladus had a more eccentric orbit in the past, the enhanced tidal forces could be sufficient to maintain a subsurface ocean, such that a periodic enhancement in
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">
        eccentricity
       </a>
       could maintain a subsurface ocean that periodically changes in size.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts2008_114-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Roberts2008-114">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A more recent analysis claimed that "a model of the tiger stripes as tidally flexed slots that puncture the ice shell can simultaneously explain the persistence of the eruptions through the tidal cycle, the phase lag, and the total power output of the tiger stripe terrain, while suggesting that eruptions are maintained over geological timescales."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kite_3972–3975_91-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kite_3972–3975-91">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Previous models suggest that resonant perturbations of Dione could provide the necessary periodic eccentricity changes to maintain the subsurface ocean of Enceladus, if the ocean contains a substantial amount of
       <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
        ammonia
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The surface of Enceladus indicates that the entire moon has experienced periods of enhanced heat flux in the past.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grl.052736_118-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-grl.052736-118">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Radioactive_heating">
        Radioactive heating
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Radioactive heating">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The "hot start" model of heating suggests Enceladus began as ice and rock that contained rapidly decaying short-lived
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive_isotopes" title="Radioactive isotopes">
        radioactive isotopes
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Aluminium-26" title="Aluminium-26">
        aluminium
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron-60" title="Iron-60">
        iron
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manganese-60" title="Manganese-60">
        manganese
       </a>
       . Enormous amounts of heat were then produced as these isotopes decayed for about 7 million years, resulting in the consolidation of rocky material at the core surrounded by a shell of ice. Although the heat from radioactivity would decrease over time, the combination of radioactivity and tidal forces from Saturn's gravitational tug could prevent the subsurface ocean from freezing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Hot_Start_on_Enceladus_106-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-A_Hot_Start_on_Enceladus-106">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The present-day radiogenic heating rate is 3.2 × 10
       <sup>
        15
       </sup>
       ergs/s (or 0.32 gigawatts), assuming Enceladus has a composition of ice, iron and silicate materials.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Heating from long-lived radioactive isotopes
       <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">
        uranium
       </a>
       -238,
       <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">
        uranium-235
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorium-232" title="Thorium-232">
        thorium
       </a>
       -232 and
       <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40">
        potassium
       </a>
       -40 inside Enceladus would add 0.3 gigawatts to the observed heat flux.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15_gigawatts_107-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-15_gigawatts-107">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The presence of Enceladus's regionally thick subsurface ocean suggests a heat flux ~10 times higher than that from radiogenic heating in the silicate core.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perry2846_86-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-perry2846-86">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">
        Chemistry
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Chemistry">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Because no
       <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
        ammonia
       </a>
       was initially found in the vented material by
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens#Instruments" title="Cassini–Huygens">
         INMS
        </a>
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        UVIS
       </i>
       , which could act as an antifreeze, it was thought such a heated, pressurized chamber would consist of nearly pure liquid water with a temperature of at least 270 K (−3 °C), because pure water requires more
       <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
        energy
       </a>
       to melt.
      </p>
      <p>
       In July 2009 it was announced that traces of ammonia had been found in the plumes during
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">
        flybys
       </a>
       in July and October 2008.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpl.2238_119-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-jpl.2238-119">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20090723-JHW_120-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NAT-20090723-JHW-120">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Reducing the freezing point of water with
       <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
        ammonia
       </a>
       would also allow for outgassing and higher
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_pressure" title="Gas pressure">
        gas pressure
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucl.84452_121-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ucl.84452-121">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and less heat required to power the water plumes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ammonia_122-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ammonia-122">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The subsurface layer heating the surface water ice could be an ammonia–water slurry at temperatures as low as 170 K (−103 °C), and thus less energy is required to produce the plume activity.
      </p>
      <p>
       However, the observed 4.7 gigawatts heat flux is enough to power the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryovolcanism" title="Cryovolcanism">
        cryovolcanism
       </a>
       without the presence of ammonia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPSC_2013_111-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-EPSC_2013-111">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ammonia_122-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ammonia-122">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Shape_and_size">
        Shape and size
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Shape and size">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Enceladus is a relatively small satellite composed of ice and rock.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
        <a href="#cite_note-123">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is a scalene
       <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">
        ellipsoid
       </a>
       in shape; its diameters, calculated from images taken by
       <i>
        Cassini's
       </i>
       ISS (Imaging Science Subsystem) instrument, are
       <span class="nowrap">
        513 km
       </span>
       between the sub- and anti-Saturnian poles,
       <span class="nowrap">
        503 km
       </span>
       between the leading and trailing hemispheres, and
       <span class="nowrap">
        497 km
       </span>
       between the north and south poles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006_6-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-Porco_Helfenstein_et_al._2006-6">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Enceladus is only one-seventh the diameter of Earth's
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       . It ranks sixth in both mass and size among the satellites of Saturn, after
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        5,150 km
       </span>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">
        Rhea
       </a>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        1,530 km
       </span>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">
        Iapetus
       </a>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        1,440 km
       </span>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">
        Dione
       </a>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        1,120 km
       </span>
       ) and
       <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">
        Tethys
       </a>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        1,050 km
       </span>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
        <a href="#cite_note-124">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007_125-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007-125">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 206.66666666667px">
        <div style="width: 206.66666666667px">
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 204.66666666667px;">

           </a>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallerytext">
          <p>
           Enceladus
           <a href="/wiki/Transit_(astronomy)" title="Transit (astronomy)">
            transiting
           </a>
           the moon
           <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
            Titan
           </a>
          </p>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 300px">
        <div style="width: 300px">
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 298px;">

           <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_Earth_Moon_Comparison.png">

           </a>
          </div>

           </a>

           </a>
           , and Enceladus
          </p>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
       <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 213.33333333333px">
        <div style="width: 213.33333333333px">
         <div class="thumb" style="width: 211.33333333333px;">
          <div style="margin:0px auto;">

           </a>
          </div>
         </div>
