          </p>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">
        Origin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Origin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span id="Mimas.E2.80.93Enceladus_paradox">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mimas–Enceladus_paradox">
        Mimas–Enceladus paradox
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Mimas–Enceladus paradox">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">
        Mimas
       </a>
       , the innermost of the round moons of Saturn and directly interior to Enceladus, is a geologically dead body, even though it should experience stronger tidal forces than Enceladus. This apparent paradox can be explained in part by temperature-dependent properties of water ice (the main constituent of the interiors of Mimas and Enceladus). The tidal heating per unit mass is given by the formula
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle q_{tid}={\frac {63\rho n^{5}r^{4}e^{2}}{38\mu Q}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <msub>
              <mi>
               q
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                t
               </mi>
               <mi>
                i
               </mi>
               <mi>
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
             </msub>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mfrac>
               <mrow>
                <mn>
                 63
                </mn>
                <mi>
                 ρ
                 <!-- ρ -->
                </mi>
                <msup>
                 <mi>
                  n
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   5
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
                <msup>
                 <mi>
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   4
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
                <msup>
                 <mi>
                  e
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
               </mrow>
               <mrow>
                <mn>
                 38
                </mn>
                <mi>
                 μ
                 <!-- μ -->
                </mi>
                <mi>
                 Q
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mfrac>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle q_{tid}={\frac {63\rho n^{5}r^{4}e^{2}}{38\mu Q}}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="q_{tid}={\frac {63\rho n^{5}r^{4}e^{2}}{38\mu Q}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4818873c5f0bf547275f14023342b5a3da6e34d9" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:17.441ex; height:6.509ex;"/>
       </span>
       , where
       <i>
        ρ
       </i>
       is the (mass) density of the satellite,
       <i>
        n
       </i>
       is its mean orbital motion,
       <i>
        r
       </i>
       is the satellite's radius,
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">
        orbital eccentricity
       </a>
       of the satellite,
       <i>
        μ
       </i>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Shear_modulus" title="Shear modulus">
        shear modulus
       </a>
       and
       <i>
        Q
       </i>
       is the dimensionless
       <a href="/wiki/Dissipation_factor" title="Dissipation factor">
        dissipation factor
       </a>
       . For a same-temperature approximation, the expected value of q
       <sub>
        tid
       </sub>
       for Mimas is about 40 times that of Enceladus. However, the material parameters
       <i>
        μ
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Q
       </i>
       are temperature dependent. At high temperatures (close to the melting point),
       <i>
        μ
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Q
       </i>
       are low, so tidal heating is high. Modeling suggests that for Enceladus, both a 'basic' low-energy thermal state with little internal temperature gradient, and an 'excited' high-energy thermal state with a significant temperature gradient, and consequent convection (endogenic geologic activity), once established, would be stable. For Mimas, only a low-energy state is expected to be stable, despite its being closer to Saturn. So the model predicts a low-internal-temperature state for Mimas (values of
       <i>
        μ
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Q
       </i>
       are high) but a possible higher-temperature state for Enceladus (values of
       <i>
        μ
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Q
       </i>
       are low).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asr.013_126-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-asr.013-126">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additional historical information is needed to explain how Enceladus first entered the high-energy state (e.g. more
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiogenic_heating" title="Radiogenic heating">
        radiogenic heating
       </a>
       or a more eccentric orbit in the past).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aj.752_127-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-aj.752-127">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The significantly higher density of Enceladus relative to Mimas (1.61 vs. 1.15 g/cm
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       ), implying a larger content of rock and more radiogenic heating in its early history, has also been cited as an important factor in resolving the Mimas paradox.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowen2006_128-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cowen2006-128">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It has been suggested that for an icy satellite the size of Mimas or Enceladus to enter an 'excited state' of tidal heating and convection, it would need to enter an orbital resonance before it lost too much of its primordial internal heat. Because Mimas, being smaller, would cool more rapidly than Enceladus, its window of opportunity for initiating orbital resonance-driven convection would have been considerably shorter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Czechowski2014_129-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Czechowski2014-129">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Proto-Enceladus_hypothesis">
        Proto-Enceladus hypothesis
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Proto-Enceladus hypothesis">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Enceladus is losing mass at a rate of 200 kg/second. If mass loss at this rate continued for 4.5 Gyr, the satellite would have lost approximately 30% of its initial mass. A similar value is obtained by assuming that the initial densities of Enceladus and Mimas were equal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Czechowski2014_129-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Czechowski2014-129">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It suggests that tectonics in the south polar region is probably mainly related to subsidence and associated subduction caused by the process of mass loss.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Czechowski2014_129-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Czechowski2014-129">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Date_of_formation">
        Date of formation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Date of formation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 2016, a study of how the orbits of Saturn's moons should have changed due to tidal effects suggested that all of Saturn's satellites inward of Titan, including Enceladus (whose geologic activity was used to derive the strength of tidal effects on Saturn's satellites), may have formed as little as 100 million years ago.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
        <a href="#cite_note-130">
         [127]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_habitability">
        Potential habitability
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Potential habitability">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Enceladus ejects plumes of salt water that are laced with grains of silica-rich sand,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobie_2015_131-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tobie_2015-131">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
       nitrogen (in ammonia),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKay_2014_132-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McKay_2014-132">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and organic molecules, including trace amounts of simple hydrocarbons such as
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        methane
       </a>
       (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        CH
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         4
        </span>
       </span>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">
        propane
       </a>
       (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        C
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         3
        </span>
        H
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         8
        </span>
       </span>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">
        acetylene
       </a>
       (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        C
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
        H
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       ) and
       <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">
        formaldehyde
       </a>
       (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        CH
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
        O
       </span>
       ), which are carbon-bearing molecules.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.5179_88-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-space.5179-88">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PR03262008_89-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-PR03262008-89">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space.com_133-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Space.com-133">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This indicates that hydrothermal activity—an energy source—may be at work in Enceladus's subsurface ocean.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobie_2015_131-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Tobie_2015-131">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hydrothermal_vents_134-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hydrothermal_vents-134">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition, models indicate the large rocky core is porous, allowing water to flow through it to pick up heat.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spotts_135-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spotts-135">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taubner_EPSC_2015_136-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Taubner_EPSC_2015-136">
         [133]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
        <a href="#cite_note-137">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Molecular hydrogen (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        H
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       ), a geochemical source of energy that can be
       <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">
        metabolized
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Methanogen" title="Methanogen">
        methanogen
       </a>
       microbes to provide energy for life, could be present if, as models suggest, Enceladus's salty ocean has an alkaline pH from
       <a href="/wiki/Serpentinite" title="Serpentinite">
        serpentinization
       </a>
       of chondritic rock.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pH_2015_81-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-pH_2015-81">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glein_2015_82-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Glein_2015-82">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall_83-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wall-83">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The presence of an internal global salty ocean with an aquatic environment supported by global ocean circulation patterns,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spotts_135-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spotts-135">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with an energy source and simple organic compounds in contact with Enceladus's rocky core,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witze2014_26-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Witze2014-26">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140404_27-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140404-27">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MckayPR_138-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MckayPR-138">
         [135]
        </a>
       </sup>
       may advance the study of
       <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">
        astrobiology
       </a>
       and the study of potentially
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">
        habitable environments
       </a>
       for microbial
       <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">
        extraterrestrial life
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140403_25-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140403-25">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ocean_Apr_2014_74-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ocean_Apr_2014-74">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian-20140403_75-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Guardian-20140403-75">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_139-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-guardian-139">
         [136]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discover_Magazine_140-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Discover_Magazine-140">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
        <a href="#cite_note-141">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The presence of a wide range of organic compounds and ammonia indicates their source may be similar to the water/rock reactions known to occur on Earth and that are known to support life.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_strategy_2015_142-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA_strategy_2015-142">
         [139]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Therefore, several robotic missions have been proposed to further explore Enceladus and assess its habitability; some of the proposed missions are:
       <a href="/wiki/Journey_to_Enceladus_and_Titan" title="Journey to Enceladus and Titan">
        Journey to Enceladus and Titan
       </a>
       (JET),
       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_Explorer" title="Enceladus Explorer">
        Enceladus Explorer
       </a>
       (En-Ex),
       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_Life_Finder" title="Enceladus Life Finder">
        Enceladus Life Finder
       </a>
       (ELF),
       <a href="/wiki/Life_Investigation_For_Enceladus" title="Life Investigation For Enceladus">
        Life Investigation For Enceladus
       </a>
       (LIFE), and
       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_Life_Signatures_and_Habitability" title="Enceladus Life Signatures and Habitability">
        Enceladus Life Signatures and Habitability
       </a>
       (ELSAH).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrothermal_vents">
        Hydrothermal vents
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Hydrothermal vents">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="4000" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg/220px-PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg/330px-PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg/440px-PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA19058-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-PossibleHydrothermalActivity-ArtistConcept-20150311.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An artist's concept of possible hydrothermal activity on Enceladus.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On April 13, 2017, NASA announced the discovery of possible hydrothermal activity on Enceladus' sub-surface ocean floor. In 2015, the Cassini probe made a close fly-by of Enceladus' south pole, flying within 48.3 km (30 mi) of the surface, as well as through a plume in the process. A mass spectrometer on the craft detected molecular hydrogen (H
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       ) from the plume, and after months of analysis, the conclusion was made that the hydrogen was most likely the result of hydrothermal activity beneath the surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
        <a href="#cite_note-143">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has been speculated that such activity could be a potential oasis of habitability.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170413_144-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170413-144">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
        <a href="#cite_note-145">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
        <a href="#cite_note-146">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The presence of ample hydrogen in Enceladus's ocean means that microbes – if any exist there – could use it to obtain energy by combining the
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
        hydrogen
       </a>
       with
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       dissolved in the
       <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
        water
       </a>
       . The chemical reaction is known as "
       <a href="/wiki/Methanogenesis" title="Methanogenesis">
        methanogenesis
       </a>
       " because it produces
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        methane
       </a>
       as a byproduct, and is at the root of the tree of life on
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Earliest_known_life_forms" title="Earliest known life forms">
        birthplace of all life that is known to exist
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
        <a href="#cite_note-147">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
        <a href="#cite_note-148">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">
        Exploration
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Exploration">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Voyager_missions">
        <i>
         Voyager
        </i>
        missions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Voyager missions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">
        Voyager program
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The two
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">
         Voyager
        </a>
       </i>
       spacecraft made the first close-up images of Enceladus.
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">
         Voyager 1
        </a>
       </i>
       was the first to fly past Enceladus, at a distance of 202,000 km on November 12, 1980.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_149-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Voyager-149">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Images acquired from this distance had very poor spatial resolution, but revealed a highly reflective surface devoid of impact craters, indicating a youthful surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terrile_150-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Terrile-150">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Voyager 1
       </i>
       also confirmed that Enceladus was embedded in the densest part of Saturn's diffuse
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#E_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">
        E ring
       </a>
       . Combined with the apparent youthful appearance of the surface,
       <i>
        Voyager
       </i>
       scientists suggested that the E ring consisted of particles vented from Enceladus's surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terrile_150-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Terrile-150">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">
         Voyager 2
        </a>
       </i>
       passed closer to Enceladus (87,010 km) on August 26, 1981, allowing higher-resolution images to be obtained.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_149-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Voyager-149">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These images showed a young surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rothery_44-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rothery-44">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They also revealed a surface with different regions with vastly different surface ages, with a heavily cratered mid- to high-northern latitude region, and a lightly cratered region closer to the equator. This geologic diversity contrasts with the ancient, heavily cratered surface of
       <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">
        Mimas
       </a>
       , another moon of Saturn slightly smaller than Enceladus. The geologically youthful terrains came as a great surprise to the scientific community, because no theory was then able to predict that such a small (and cold, compared to
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       's highly active moon
       <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">
        Io
       </a>
       ) celestial body could bear signs of such activity.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cassini">
        <i>
         Cassini
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Cassini">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
        Cassini–Huygens
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tnone center">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:836px;max-width:836px">
        <div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent">

         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20151030-flyby_151-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151030-flyby-151">
           [148]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
         <div class="thumbimage">

          </center>

          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">
          <center>
           Up close
          </center>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA17202-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-FlybyPlumes-20151028.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="1041" data-file-width="1041" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/PIA17202-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-FlybyPlumes-20151028.jpg/200px-PIA17202-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-FlybyPlumes-20151028.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/PIA17202-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-FlybyPlumes-20151028.jpg/300px-PIA17202-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-FlybyPlumes-20151028.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/PIA17202-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-FlybyPlumes-20151028.jpg/400px-PIA17202-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-FlybyPlumes-20151028.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">
          <center>
           Plumes
          </center>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA17205-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-AfterFlyby-20151028.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="765" data-file-width="837" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/PIA17205-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-AfterFlyby-20151028.jpg/220px-PIA17205-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-AfterFlyby-20151028.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/PIA17205-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-AfterFlyby-20151028.jpg/330px-PIA17205-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-AfterFlyby-20151028.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/PIA17205-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-AfterFlyby-20151028.jpg/440px-PIA17205-SaturnMoon-Enceladus-AfterFlyby-20151028.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:center">
          <center>
           After
          </center>
         </div>
        </div>

      <p>
       The answers to many remaining mysteries of Enceladus had to wait until the arrival of the
       <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
        <i>
         Cassini
        </i>
        spacecraft
       </a>
       on July 1, 2004, when it entered orbit around Saturn. Given the results from the
       <i>
        Voyager 2
       </i>
       images, Enceladus was considered a priority target by the
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       mission planners, and several targeted
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">
        flybys
       </a>
       within 1,500 km of the surface were planned as well as numerous, "non-targeted" opportunities within 100,000 km of Enceladus. The flybys have yielded significant information concerning Enceladus's surface, as well as the discovery of
       <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">
        water vapor
       </a>
       with traces of simple
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">
        hydrocarbons
       </a>
       venting from the geologically active south polar region. These discoveries prompted the adjustment of
       <i>
        Cassini's
       </i>
       flight plan to allow closer flybys of Enceladus, including an encounter in March 2008 that took it to within 48 km of the surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
        <a href="#cite_note-154">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Cassini's
       </i>
       extended mission included seven close flybys of Enceladus between July 2008 and July 2010, including two passes at only 50 km in the later half of 2008.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpaceDaily02052007_155-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SpaceDaily02052007-155">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       performed a flyby on October 28, 2015, passing as close as 49 km (30 mi) and through a plume.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-press-release_156-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa-press-release-156">
         [153]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Confirmation of molecular hydrogen (
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        H
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       ) would be an independent line of evidence that hydrothermal activity is taking place in the Enceladus seafloor, increasing its habitability.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall_83-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wall-83">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       has provided strong evidence that Enceladus has an ocean with an energy source, nutrients and organic molecules, making Enceladus one of the best places for the study of potentially
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">
        habitable environments
       </a>
       for extraterrestrial life.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LCPM_Enceladus_157-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LCPM_Enceladus-157">
         [154]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ciclops1881_158-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ciclops1881-158">
         [155]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By contrast, the water thought to be on Jupiter's moon
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">
        Europa
       </a>
       is located under a much thicker layer of ice.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
        <a href="#cite_note-159">
         [156]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_mission_concepts">
        Proposed mission concepts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Enceladus&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Proposed mission concepts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The discoveries
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       has made at Enceladus have prompted studies into follow-up mission concepts, including a flyby plume sample-return mission by NASA,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKay_2014_132-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-McKay_2014-132">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LCPM_Enceladus_157-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-LCPM_Enceladus-157">
         [154]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a probe flyby (
       <a href="/wiki/Journey_to_Enceladus_and_Titan" title="Journey to Enceladus and Titan">
        Journey to Enceladus and Titan
       </a>
       or JET) to analyze plume contents
       <i>
        in-situ
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sotin2011_160-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sotin2011-160">
         [157]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JET_flyby_mission_161-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-JET_flyby_mission-161">
         [158]
        </a>
       </sup>
       a lander by the
       <a href="/wiki/German_Aerospace_Center" title="German Aerospace Center">
        German Aerospace Center
       </a>
       to study the habitability potential of its subsurface ocean (
       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_Explorer" title="Enceladus Explorer">
        Enceladus Explorer
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Konstantinidis_2015_162-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Konstantinidis_2015-162">
         [159]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ut.93879_163-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ut.93879-163">
         [160]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dlr.4874_164-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-dlr.4874-164">
         [161]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and two astrobiology-oriented mission concepts (the
       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_Life_Finder" title="Enceladus Life Finder">
        Enceladus Life Finder
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2015_LPSC_165-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-2015_LPSC-165">
         [162]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-contestants_166-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-contestants-166">
         [163]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Life_Investigation_For_Enceladus" title="Life Investigation For Enceladus">
        Life Investigation For Enceladus
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Interview2012_167-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Interview2012-167">
         [164]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Astro2012_168-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Astro2012-168">
         [165]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">
        European Space Agency
       </a>
       (ESA) is also assessing concepts to send a probe to Enceladus in a mission to be combined with studies of Titan:
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_Saturn_System_Mission#Origin_and_status" title="Titan Saturn System Mission">
        <i>
         TandEM
        </i>
       </a>
       (Titan and Enceladus Mission).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TandEM_169-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-TandEM-169">
         [166]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Additionally, the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Titan_Saturn_System_Mission" title="Titan Saturn System Mission">
         Titan Saturn System Mission
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        TSSM
       </i>
       ) was a joint
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       /
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">
        ESA
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Flagship_Program" title="Flagship Program">
        flagship-class
       </a>
       proposal for exploration of
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">
        Saturn's moons
       </a>
       , with a focus on Enceladus.
       <i>
        TSSM
       </i>
       was competing against the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europa_Jupiter_System_Mission" title="Europa Jupiter System Mission">
         Europa Jupiter System Mission
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        EJSM
       </i>
       ) proposal for funding. In February 2009, it was announced that NASA/ESA had given the
       <i>
        EJSM
       </i>
       mission priority ahead of
       <i>
        TSSM
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.7897585_170-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bbc.7897585-170">
         [167]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although
       <i>
        TSSM
       </i>
       will continue to be studied and evaluated.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        2006 GSFC NASA Academy: 'EAGLE' study
       </li>
       <li>

       </li>

        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_and_Enceladus_Mission#Origin_and_status" title="Titan and Enceladus Mission">
         Titan and Enceladus Mission
        </a>
        (TandEM)
       </li>
       <li>
        2007
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JPL" title="JPL">
         JPL
        </a>
        : Enceladus RMA Study
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ieee.5747289_171-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-ieee.5747289-171">
          [168]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        2008 NASA/ESA:
        <a href="/wiki/Titan_Saturn_System_Mission" title="Titan Saturn System Mission">
         Titan Saturn System Mission
        </a>
        (TSSM)
       </li>
       <li>
        2010 PSDS Decadal Survey: Enceladus Studies including the Enceladus Orbiter
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172">
         <a href="#cite_note-172">
          [169]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        2010 JPL:
        <a href="/wiki/Journey_to_Enceladus_and_Titan" title="Journey to Enceladus and Titan">
         Journey to Enceladus and Titan
        </a>
        (JET)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JET_204_173-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-JET_204-173">
          [170]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        2012
        <a href="/wiki/German_Aerospace_Center" title="German Aerospace Center">
         DLR
        </a>
        :
        <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_Explorer" title="Enceladus Explorer">
         Enceladus Explorer
        </a>
        (EnEx) lander, employing the
        <a href="/wiki/IceMole" title="IceMole">
         IceMole
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
         <a href="#cite_note-174">
          [171]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        2012 JPL:
        <a href="/wiki/Life_Investigation_For_Enceladus" title="Life Investigation For Enceladus">
         Life Investigation For Enceladus
        </a>
        (LIFE)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Interview2012_167-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Interview2012-167">
          [164]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Porco_2014_175-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Porco_2014-175">
          [172]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doc_2013_176-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Doc_2013-176">
          [173]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        2015 JPL:
        <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_Life_Finder" title="Enceladus Life Finder">
         Enceladus Life Finder
        </a>
        (ELF)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
         <a href="#cite_note-177">
          [174]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        2017:
        <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_Life_Signatures_and_Habitability" title="Enceladus Life Signatures and Habitability">
         Enceladus Life Signatures and Habitability
        </a>
        (ELSAH)
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       In November 2017, Russian billionaire
       <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Milner" title="Yuri Milner">
        Yuri Milner
       </a>
       expressed interest in funding a "low-cost, privately funded mission to Enceladus which can be launched relatively soon."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
        <a href="#cite_note-178">
         [175]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Speaking at the New Space Age conference in
       <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">
        Seattle
       </a>
       , Milner described Enceladus as "the most promising candidate” for extraterrestrial life in the solar system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179">
        <a href="#cite_note-179">
         [176]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      </h2>
      <p>
       <b>
        Explanatory notes
       </b>
      </p>
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-caption-1">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-caption_1-0">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           This image of Enceladus by
           <i>
            Cassini
           </i>
           , taken during its flyby of October 28, 2015, shows the younger terrain of
           <a href="/wiki/Sarandib_Planitia" title="Sarandib Planitia">
            Sarandib
           </a>
           and
           <a href="/wiki/Diyar_Planitia" title="Diyar Planitia">
            Diyar Planitia
           </a>
           , populated with many grooves (
           <a href="/wiki/Sulcus_(geology)" title="Sulcus (geology)">
            sulci
           </a>
           ) and depressions (
           <a href="/wiki/Fossa_(geology)" title="Fossa (geology)">
            fossae
           </a>
           ). Older, cratered terrain can be seen towards Enceladus's north pole. The prominent feature visible near the south pole is Cashmere Sulci.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-adjectives-4">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-adjectives_4-0">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Used with roughly equal frequency
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-crater_flux-53">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-crater_flux_53-0">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Without samples to provide absolute age determinations, crater counting is currently the only method for determining surface age on most planetary surfaces. Unfortunately, there is currently disagreement in the scientific community regarding the flux of impactors in the outer Solar System. These competing models can significantly alter the age estimate even with the same crater counts. For the sake of completeness, both age estimates from
           <a href="#CITEREFPorco,_Helfenstein_et_al._2006">
            Porco, Helfenstein et al. 2006
           </a>
           are provided.
          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=New+Scientist&amp;rft.atitle=Private+mission+may+get+us+back+to+Enceladus+sooner+than+NASA&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.newscientist.com%2Farticle%2Fmg23631533-900-private-mission-may-get-us-back-to-enceladus-sooner-than-nasa%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEnceladus">
           <span style="display:none;">

          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
